	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Celts</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>

		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the ancient and medieval peoples of Europe. For Celts of the present day, see <a href="/wiki/Celts_(modern)" title="Celts (modern)">Celts (modern)</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Celt_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Celt (disambiguation)">Celt (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

<p>The <b>Celts</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/ɛ/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">ɛ</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/ɛ/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">ɛ</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>, see <a href="/wiki/Names_of_the_Celts#Pronunciation" title="Names of the Celts">pronunciation of <i>Celt</i></a> for different usages) are<sup id="cite_ref-WM_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WM-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> a collection of <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" title="Ethnic group">ethnic groups</a> of <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> identified by their use of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic languages</a><sup id="cite_ref-EB_Celt_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB_Celt-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> and cultural similarities.<sup id="cite_ref-Koch_encyclopedia_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koch_encyclopedia-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>  The history of <a href="/wiki/Pre-Celtic" title="Pre-Celtic">pre-Celtic</a> Europe and the exact relationship between ethnic, linguistic and cultural factors in the Celtic world remains uncertain and controversial.<sup id="cite_ref-SJames_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SJames-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The exact geographic spread of the ancient Celts is disputed; in particular, the ways in which the Iron Age inhabitants of Great Britain and Ireland should be regarded as Celts have become a subject of controversy.<sup id="cite_ref-Koch_encyclopedia_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koch_encyclopedia-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SJames_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SJames-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JCollis_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JCollis-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FPryor_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FPryor-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> According to one theory, the common root of the Celtic languages, the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Celtic_language" title="Proto-Celtic language">Proto-Celtic language</a>, arose in the Late Bronze Age <a href="/wiki/Urnfield_culture" title="Urnfield culture">Urnfield culture</a> of Central Europe, which flourished from around 1200 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-ChadCorc_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChadCorc-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a theory proposed in the 19th century, the first people to adopt cultural characteristics regarded as Celtic were the people of the Iron Age <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt_culture" title="Hallstatt culture">Hallstatt culture</a> in central Europe (c. 800–450 BC), named for the rich grave finds in <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt" title="Hallstatt">Hallstatt</a>, Austria.<sup id="cite_ref-ChadCorc_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChadCorc-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Thus this area is sometimes called the "Celtic homeland". By or during the later <a href="/wiki/La_T%C3%A8ne_culture" title="La Tène culture">La Tène</a> period (c. 450 BC to the Roman conquest), this Celtic culture was supposed to have expanded by <a href="/wiki/Trans-cultural_diffusion" title="Trans-cultural diffusion">trans-cultural diffusion</a> or <a href="/wiki/Human_migration" title="Human migration">migration</a> to the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> (<a href="/wiki/Insular_Celts" title="Insular Celts">Insular Celts</a>), France and the <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">Low Countries</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gauls" title="Gauls">Gauls</a>), <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a>, Poland and much of Central Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> (<a href="/wiki/Celtiberians" title="Celtiberians">Celtiberians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celtici" title="Celtici">Celtici</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lusitanians" title="Lusitanians">Lusitanians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gallaeci" title="Gallaeci">Gallaeci</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Italy" title="Northern Italy">northern Italy</a> (<a href="/wiki/Golasecca_culture" title="Golasecca culture">Golasecca culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cisalpine_Gaul" title="Cisalpine Gaul">Cisalpine Gauls</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-koch2010_core_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-koch2010_core-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> and, following the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_settlement_of_Eastern_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic settlement of Eastern Europe">Celtic settlement of Eastern Europe</a> beginning in <a href="/wiki/279_BC" title="279 BC">279 BC</a>, as far east as central <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Galatia" title="Galatia">Galatians</a>) in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-koch2010_expansion_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-koch2010_expansion-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest undisputed direct examples of a Celtic language are the <a href="/wiki/Lepontic_language" title="Lepontic language">Lepontic</a> inscriptions beginning in the <a href="/wiki/6th_century_BC" title="6th century BC">6th century BC</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Stifter_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stifter-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Continental_Celtic_languages" title="Continental Celtic languages">Continental Celtic languages</a> are attested almost exclusively through inscriptions and place-names. <a href="/wiki/Insular_Celtic_languages" title="Insular Celtic languages">Insular Celtic languages</a> are attested beginning around the <a href="/wiki/4th_century" title="4th century">4th century</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ogham_inscription" title="Ogham inscription">Ogham inscriptions</a>, although they were clearly being spoken much earlier. Celtic literary tradition begins with <a href="/wiki/Old_Irish" title="Old Irish">Old Irish</a> texts around the <a href="/wiki/8th_century" title="8th century">8th century</a> AD. Coherent texts of <a href="/wiki/Early_Irish_literature" title="Early Irish literature">Early Irish literature</a>, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/T%C3%A1in_B%C3%B3_C%C3%BAailnge" title="Táin Bó Cúailnge">Táin Bó Cúailnge</a></i> ("<a href="/wiki/Cattle_raiding" title="Cattle raiding">Cattle Raid</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cooley_Peninsula" title="Cooley Peninsula">Cooley</a>"), survive in <a href="/wiki/12th-century" class="mw-redirect" title="12th-century">12th-century</a> <a href="/wiki/Recension" title="Recension">recensions</a>.
</p><p>By the mid-<a href="/wiki/1st_millennium" title="1st millennium">1st millennium</a>, with the expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> and migrating <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic</a> tribes, Celtic culture and <a href="/wiki/Insular_Celtic_languages" title="Insular Celtic languages">Insular Celtic languages</a> had become restricted to Ireland, the western and northern parts of Great Britain (<a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, Scotland, and <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>. Between the 5th and 8th centuries, the Celtic-speaking communities in these Atlantic regions emerged as a reasonably cohesive cultural entity. They had a common linguistic, religious and artistic heritage that distinguished them from the culture of the surrounding polities.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> By the 6th century, however, the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Celtic_languages" title="Continental Celtic languages">Continental Celtic languages</a> were no longer in wide use.
</p><p>Insular Celtic culture diversified into that of the <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaels</a> (<a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scottish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manx_people" title="Manx people">Manx</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Britons" title="Celtic Britons">Celtic Britons</a> (<a href="/wiki/Welsh_people" title="Welsh people">Welsh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornish_people" title="Cornish people">Cornish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bretons" title="Bretons">Bretons</a>) of the medieval and modern periods.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> A modern <a href="/wiki/Celtic_identity" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic identity">Celtic identity</a> was constructed as part of the Romanticist <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Revival" title="Celtic Revival">Celtic Revival</a> in Great Britain, Ireland, and other European territories, such as <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galicia_(Spain)" title="Galicia (Spain)">Spanish Galicia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Today, <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">Breton</a> are still spoken in parts of their historical territories, and <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">Manx</a> are undergoing a revival.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names_and_terminology">Names and terminology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:131px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Galician_Celtic_Stele_-_Estela_Galaica.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c9/Galician_Celtic_Stele_-_Estela_Galaica.jpg" decoding="async" width="129" height="454" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="129" data-file-height="454" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Galician_Celtic_Stele_-_Estela_Galaica.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Celtic <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stele</a> from <a href="/wiki/Galicia_(Spain)" title="Galicia (Spain)">Galicia</a>, 2nd century:<small> <b>"APANA·AMBO</b>(-)&#160;/<br /><b>LLI·F</b>(<i>ilia</i>)<b>·CELTICA</b>&#160;/<br /><a href="/wiki/Celtici_Supertamarici" title="Celtici Supertamarici"><b>SUPERTAM</b>(<i>arica</i>)</a>&#160;/<br />(<i>castello</i>) <b>MAIOBRI&#160;/<br /> AN</b>(<i>norum</i>)<b>·XXV·<br />H</b>(<i>ic</i>)<b>·S</b>(<i>ita</i>)<b>·E</b>(<i>st</i>)&#160;/<br /> <b>APANUS·FR</b>(<i>ater</i>)<b>·<br />F</b>(<i>aciendum</i>)<b>·C</b>(<i>uravit</i>)"</small></div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Names_of_the_Celts" title="Names of the Celts">Names of the Celts</a></div>
<p>The first recorded use of the name of Celts – as <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text"><b>Κελτοί</b></span> (<i>Keltoi</i>) in Greek – to refer to an ethnic group was by <a href="/wiki/Hecataeus_of_Miletus" title="Hecataeus of Miletus">Hecataeus of Miletus</a>, the Greek geographer, in 517 BC,<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> when writing about a people living near Massilia (modern <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseille</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In the fifth century BC, <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> referred to <i>Keltoi</i> living around the head of the Danube and also in the far west of Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The etymology of the term <i>Keltoi</i> is unclear. Possible roots include Indo-European *<i>kʲel</i> 'to hide' (present also in Old Irish <i>ceilid</i>), IE *<i>kʲel</i> 'to heat' or *<i>kel</i> 'to impel'.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Several authors have supposed it to be Celtic in origin, while others view it as a name coined by Greeks. Linguist Patrizia De Bernardo Stempel falls in the latter group, and suggests the meaning "the tall ones".<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/1st_century_BC" title="1st century BC">1st century BC</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> reported that the people known to the Romans as <a href="/wiki/Gauls" title="Gauls">Gauls</a> (<a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Galli</i>) called themselves Celts,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> which suggests that even if the name <i>Keltoi</i> was bestowed by the Greeks, it had been adopted to some extent as a collective name by the tribes of Gaul. The geographer Strabo, writing about Gaul towards the end of the first century BC, refers to the "race which is now called both Gallic and Galatic," though he also uses the term Celtica as a synonym for Gaul, which is separated from Iberia by the Pyrenees. Yet he reports Celtic peoples in Iberia, and also uses the ethnic names Celtiberi and Celtici for peoples there, as distinct from Lusitani and Iberi.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> cited the use of Celtici in <a href="/wiki/Lusitania" title="Lusitania">Lusitania</a> as a tribal surname,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> which epigraphic findings have confirmed.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Latin <i><b>Gallus</b></i> (pl. <i>Galli</i>) might stem from a Celtic ethnic or <a href="/wiki/Tribal_name" title="Tribal name">tribal name</a> originally, perhaps one borrowed into Latin during the Celtic expansions into Italy during the early fifth century BC. Its root may be the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Celtic_language" title="Proto-Celtic language">Proto-Celtic</a> <i>*galno</i>, meaning "power, strength", hence Old Irish <i>gal</i> "boldness, ferocity" and Welsh <i>gallu</i> "to be able, power". The tribal names of <b><a href="/wiki/Gallaeci" title="Gallaeci">Gallaeci</a></b> and the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <b><a href="/wiki/Galatians_(people)" title="Galatians (people)">Γαλάται</a></b> (<i>Galatai</i>, Latinized <i>Galatae</i>; see the region <a href="/wiki/Galatia" title="Galatia">Galatia</a> in Anatolia) most probably have the same origin.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The suffix <i>-atai</i> might be an Ancient Greek inflection.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Classical writers did not apply the terms <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">Κελτοί</span> (<i>Keltoi</i>) or <i>Celtae</i> to the inhabitants of Britain or Ireland,<sup id="cite_ref-Koch_encyclopedia_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koch_encyclopedia-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SJames_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SJames-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JCollis_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JCollis-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> which has led to some scholars preferring not to use the term for the Iron Age inhabitants of those islands.<sup id="cite_ref-Koch_encyclopedia_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koch_encyclopedia-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SJames_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SJames-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JCollis_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JCollis-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FPryor_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FPryor-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Celt</b> is a modern English word, first attested in 1707, in the writing of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Lhuyd" title="Edward Lhuyd">Edward Lhuyd</a>, whose work, along with that of other late 17th-century scholars, brought academic attention to the languages and history of the early Celtic inhabitants of Great Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The English form <b>Gaul</b> (first recorded in the 17th century) and <i><b>Gaulish</b></i> come from the French <i>Gaule</i> and <i>Gaulois</i>, a borrowing from <a href="/wiki/Frankish_language" title="Frankish language">Frankish</a> <i>*Walholant</i>, "Roman land" (see <a href="/wiki/Gaul#Name" title="Gaul">Gaul: Name</a>), the root of which is <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">Proto-Germanic</a> <i>*<a href="/wiki/Walhaz" title="Walhaz">walha-</a></i>, "foreigner, Roman, Celt", whence the English word <b>Welsh</b> (<a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <i>wælisċ</i> &lt; *<i>walhiska-</i>), South German <i><a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Welsche" class="extiw" title="de:Welsche">welsch</a></i>, meaning "Celtic speaker", "French speaker" or "Italian speaker" in different contexts, and Old Norse <i>valskr</i>, pl. <i>valir</i>, "Gaulish, French"). Proto-Germanic <i>*walha</i> is derived ultimately from the name of the <a href="/wiki/Volcae" title="Volcae">Volcae</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> a Celtic tribe who lived first in the south of Germany and in central Europe and then migrated to Gaul.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> This means that English Gaul, despite its superficial similarity, is not actually derived from Latin <i>Gallia</i> (which should have produced <i>**Jaille</i> in French), though it does refer to the same ancient region.
</p><p><b>Celtic</b> refers to a <a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">family of languages</a> and, more generally, means "of the Celts" or "in the style of the Celts". Several archaeological cultures are considered Celtic in nature, based on unique sets of artefacts. The link between language and artefact is aided by the presence of inscriptions.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The relatively modern idea of an identifiable Celtic <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultural</a> identity or "Celticity" generally focuses on similarities among languages, works of art, and classical texts,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> and sometimes also among material artefacts, <a href="/wiki/Social_organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Social organisation">social organisation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Homeland" title="Homeland">homeland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Celtic_mythology" title="Celtic mythology">mythology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Earlier theories held that these similarities suggest a common racial origin for the various Celtic peoples, but more recent theories hold that they reflect a common cultural and language heritage more than a genetic one. Celtic cultures seem to have been widely diverse, with the use of a Celtic language being the main thing they had in common.<sup id="cite_ref-Koch_encyclopedia_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koch_encyclopedia-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, the term Celtic generally refers to the languages and respective cultures of Ireland, Scotland, Wales, <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>, also known as the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_nations" title="Celtic nations">Celtic nations</a>. These are the regions where four Celtic languages are still spoken to some extent as mother tongues. The four are <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish Gaelic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">Breton</a>; plus two recent revivals, <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a> (one of the <a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">Brittonic languages</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">Manx</a> (one of the <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">Goidelic languages</a>). There are also attempts to reconstruct <a href="/wiki/Cumbric" title="Cumbric">Cumbric</a>, a Brittonic language from <a href="/wiki/North_West_England" title="North West England">North West England</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_West_Scotland" title="South West Scotland">South West Scotland</a>. Celtic regions of <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">Continental Europe</a> are those whose residents claim a Celtic heritage, but where no Celtic language has survived; these areas include the western <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> and north-central <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> (<a href="/wiki/Galicia_(Spain)" title="Galicia (Spain)">Galicia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asturias" title="Asturias">Asturias</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cantabria" title="Cantabria">Cantabria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Castile_and_Le%C3%B3n" title="Castile and León">Castile and León</a>, <a href="/wiki/Extremadura" title="Extremadura">Extremadura</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Continental Celts</b> are the Celtic-speaking people of mainland Europe and <b>Insular Celts</b> are the Celtic-speaking peoples of the British and Irish islands and their descendants. The Celts of Brittany derive their language from migrating insular Celts, mainly from Wales and <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>, and so are grouped accordingly.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:372px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hallstatt_LaTene.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Hallstatt_LaTene.png/370px-Hallstatt_LaTene.png" decoding="async" width="370" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Hallstatt_LaTene.png/555px-Hallstatt_LaTene.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ee/Hallstatt_LaTene.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="438" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hallstatt_LaTene.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Overview of the <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt_culture" title="Hallstatt culture">Hallstatt</a> and  <a href="/wiki/La_T%C3%A8ne_culture" title="La Tène culture">La Tène</a> cultures.<small> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Pre-Celtic" title="Pre-Celtic">Pre-Celtic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Celticization" class="mw-redirect" title="Celticization">Celticization</a></div>

title="Havranok">Havranok</a>, Slovakia<br />(second–first century BC)</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic languages</a> form a branch of the larger <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_family" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-European family">Indo-European family</a>. By the time speakers of Celtic languages entered history around <a href="/wiki/400_BC" title="400 BC">400 BC</a>, they were already split into several language groups, and spread over much of Western continental Europe, the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>, Ireland and Britain.
The Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Ephorus" title="Ephorus">Ephorus</a> of Cyme in <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>, writing in the <a href="/wiki/4th_century_BC" title="4th century BC">4th century BC</a>, believed that the Celts came from the islands off the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> and were "driven from their homes by the frequency of wars and the violent rising of the sea".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hallstatt_culture">Hallstatt culture</span></h3>
<p>Some scholars think that the <a href="/wiki/Urnfield_culture" title="Urnfield culture">Urnfield culture</a> of western <a href="/wiki/Middle_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Europe">Middle Europe</a> represents an origin for the Celts as a distinct cultural branch of the Indo-European family.<sup id="cite_ref-ChadCorc_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChadCorc-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> This culture was preeminent in central Europe during the late <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Circa" title="Circa">circa</a> 1200 BC until 700 BC, itself following the <a href="/wiki/Unetice_culture" title="Unetice culture">Unetice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tumulus_cultures" class="mw-redirect" title="Tumulus cultures">Tumulus cultures</a>. The Urnfield period saw a dramatic increase in population in the region, probably due to innovations in technology and agriculture.
</p><p>The spread of <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">iron-working</a> led to the development of the <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt_culture" title="Hallstatt culture">Hallstatt culture</a> directly from the Urnfield (c. 700 to 500 BC). Proto-Celtic, the latest <a href="/wiki/Common_descent" title="Common descent">common ancestor</a> of all known Celtic languages, is considered by this school of thought to have been spoken at the time of the late Urnfield or early Hallstatt cultures, in the early 1st millennium BC. The spread of the Celtic languages to Iberia, Ireland and Britain would have occurred during the first half of the 1st millennium BC, the earliest <a href="/wiki/Chariot_burial" title="Chariot burial">chariot burials</a> in Britain dating to c. 500 BC. Other scholars see Celtic languages as covering Britain and Ireland, and parts of the Continent, long before any evidence of "Celtic" culture is found in archaeology. Over the centuries the language(s) developed into the separate <a href="/wiki/Celtiberian_language" title="Celtiberian language">Celtiberian</a>, Goidelic and Brittonic languages.
</p><p>The Hallstatt culture was succeeded by the <a href="/wiki/La_T%C3%A8ne_culture" title="La Tène culture">La Tène culture</a> of central Europe, which was overrun by the Roman Empire, though traces of La Tène style are still to be seen in <a href="/wiki/Gallo-Roman_culture" title="Gallo-Roman culture">Gallo-Roman artefacts</a>. In Britain and Ireland La Tène style in art survived precariously to re-emerge in <a href="/wiki/Insular_art" title="Insular art">Insular art</a>. Early <a href="/wiki/Irish_literature" title="Irish literature">Irish literature</a> casts light on the flavour and tradition of the heroic warrior elites who dominated Celtic societies. Celtic <a href="/wiki/Old_European_hydronymy" title="Old European hydronymy">river-names</a> are found in great numbers around the upper reaches of the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a> and Rhine, which led many Celtic scholars to place the <a href="/wiki/Ethnogenesis" title="Ethnogenesis">ethnogenesis</a> of the Celts in this area.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> both suggest that the heartland of the people they called Celts was in <a href="/wiki/Southern_France" title="Southern France">southern France</a>. The former says that the Gauls were to the north of the Celts, but that the Romans referred to both as Gauls (in linguistic terms the Gauls were certainly Celts). Before the discoveries at Hallstatt and La Tène, it was generally considered that the Celtic heartland was southern France, see <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">Encyclopædia Britannica</a> for 1813.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atlantic_seaboard_theory">Atlantic seaboard theory</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Myles_Dillon" title="Myles Dillon">Myles Dillon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nora_Kershaw_Chadwick" class="mw-redirect" title="Nora Kershaw Chadwick">Nora Kershaw Chadwick</a> accepted that "the Celtic settlement of the British Isles" might have to be dated to the <a href="/wiki/Beaker_culture" title="Beaker culture">Bell Beaker culture</a> concluding that "There is no reason why so early a date for the coming of the Celts should be impossible".<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cunliffewest_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cunliffewest-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mart%C3%ADn_Almagro_Gorbea" title="Martín Almagro Gorbea">Martín Almagro Gorbea</a><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> proposed the origins of the Celts could be traced back to the 3rd millennium BC, also seeking the initial roots in the Beaker period, thus offering the wide dispersion of the Celts throughout western Europe, as well as the variability of the different Celtic peoples, and the existence of ancestral traditions and ancient perspective. Using a multidisciplinary approach, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Alberto_J._Lorrio&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Alberto J. Lorrio (page does not exist)">Alberto J. Lorrio</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Gonzalo_Ruiz_Zapatero&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Gonzalo Ruiz Zapatero (page does not exist)">Gonzalo Ruiz Zapatero</a> reviewed and built on Almagro Gorbea's work to present a model for the origin of the Celtic archaeological groups in the Iberian Peninsula (Celtiberian, <a href="/wiki/Vettones" title="Vettones">Vetton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vaccaei" title="Vaccaei">Vaccean</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Castro_culture" title="Castro culture">Castro culture</a> of the northwest, <a href="/wiki/Astures" title="Astures">Asturian</a>-<a href="/wiki/Cantabri" title="Cantabri">Cantabrian</a> and Celtic of the southwest) and proposing a rethinking of the meaning of "Celtic" from a European perspective.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> More recently, John Koch<sup id="cite_ref-Koch2009_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koch2009-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Barry_Cunliffe" title="Barry Cunliffe">Barry Cunliffe</a><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> have suggested that Celtic origins lie with the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Bronze_Age" title="Atlantic Bronze Age">Atlantic Bronze Age</a>, roughly contemporaneous with the Hallstatt culture but positioned considerably to the West, extending along the Atlantic coast of Europe.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Stephen_Oppenheimer" title="Stephen Oppenheimer">Stephen Oppenheimer</a><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> points out that the only written evidence that locates the Keltoi near the source of the Danube (i.e. in the Hallstatt region) is in the <i><a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">Histories</a></i> of Herodotus. However, Oppenheimer shows that Herodotus seemed to believe the Danube rose near the <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees</a>, which would place the Ancient Celts in a region which is more in agreement with later classical writers and historians (i.e. in Gaul and the Iberian peninsula).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Linguistic_evidence">Linguistic evidence</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proto-Celtic_language" title="Proto-Celtic language">Proto-Celtic language</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Celtic_toponymy" title="Celtic toponymy">Celtic toponymy</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Proto-Celtic_language" title="Proto-Celtic language">Proto-Celtic language</a> is usually dated to the Late Bronze Age.<sup id="cite_ref-ChadCorc_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChadCorc-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The earliest records of a Celtic language are the <a href="/wiki/Lepontic" class="mw-redirect" title="Lepontic">Lepontic</a> inscriptions of <a href="/wiki/Cisalpine_Gaul" title="Cisalpine Gaul">Cisalpine Gaul</a> (Northern Italy), the oldest of which predate the <a href="/wiki/La_T%C3%A8ne_period" class="mw-redirect" title="La Tène period">La Tène period</a>. Other early inscriptions, appearing from the early La Tène period in the area of <a href="/wiki/Marseilles" class="mw-redirect" title="Marseilles">Massilia</a>, are in <a href="/wiki/Gaulish" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaulish">Gaulish</a>, which was written in the <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek alphabet</a> until the Roman conquest. <a href="/wiki/Celtiberian_language" title="Celtiberian language">Celtiberian</a> inscriptions, using their own Iberian script, appear later, after about 200 BC. Evidence of <a href="/wiki/Insular_Celtic" class="mw-redirect" title="Insular Celtic">Insular Celtic</a> is available only from about 400 AD, in the form of <a href="/wiki/Primitive_Irish" title="Primitive Irish">Primitive Irish</a> <a href="/wiki/Ogham_inscriptions" class="mw-redirect" title="Ogham inscriptions">Ogham inscriptions</a>.
</p><p>Besides epigraphical evidence, an important source of information on early Celtic is <a href="/wiki/Toponymy" title="Toponymy">toponymy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_evidence">Genetic evidence</span></h3>
<p>Historically many scholars postulated that there was genetic evidence of a common origin of the European Atlantic populations i.e.: Orkney Islands, Scottish, Irish, British, Bretons, Iberians (Basques, Galicians), <a href="/wiki/Guanches" title="Guanches">Guanches</a> and <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berbers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>More recent genetic evidence does not support the notion of a significant genetic link between these populations, beyond the fact that they are all West Eurasians. Sardinian-like Neolithic farmers did populate Britain (and all of Northern Europe) during the Neolithic period, however, recent genetics research has claimed that, between 2400BC and 2000BC, over 90% of British DNA was overturned by a North European population of ultimate Russian Steppe origin as part of an ongoing migration process that brought large amounts of Steppe DNA (including the R1b haplogroup) to North and West Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Olalde_et_al._45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olalde_et_al.-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Modern autosomal genetic clustering is testament to this fact, as both modern and Iron Age British and Irish samples cluster genetically very closely with other North European populations, not Iberians, Galicians, Basques or those from the south of France.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Such findings have largely put to rest the theory that there is a significant genetic link (beyond being Europeans) between the various 'Celtic' peoples in the Atlantic area.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeological_evidence">Archaeological evidence</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age_Europe" title="Iron Age Europe">Iron Age Europe</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hallstatt_culture.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Hallstatt_culture.png/330px-Hallstatt_culture.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Hallstatt_culture.png/495px-Hallstatt_culture.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/48/Hallstatt_culture.png 2x" data-file-width="538" data-file-height="320" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hallstatt_culture.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Hallstatt culture</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herodotus_world_map-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Herodotus_world_map-en.svg/330px-Herodotus_world_map-en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="199" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Herodotus_world_map-en.svg/495px-Herodotus_world_map-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Herodotus_world_map-en.svg/660px-Herodotus_world_map-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="788" data-file-height="476" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herodotus_world_map-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The world according to Herodotus</div></div></div>
<p>Before the 19th century, scholars<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (November 2011)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> assumed that the original land of the Celts was west of the Rhine, more precisely in Gaul, because it was where Greek and Roman ancient sources, namely Caesar, located the Celts. This view was challenged by the 19th-century historian <a href="/wiki/Marie_Henri_d%27Arbois_de_Jubainville" title="Marie Henri d&#39;Arbois de Jubainville">Marie Henri d'Arbois de Jubainville</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> who placed the land of origin of the Celts east of the Rhine. Jubainville based his arguments on a phrase of Herodotus' that placed the Celts at the source of the Danube, and argued that Herodotus had meant to place the Celtic homeland in southern Germany.
The finding of the prehistoric cemetery of Hallstat in 1846 by Johan Ramsauer and the finding of the archaeological site of La Tène by Hansli Kopp in 1857 drew attention to this area.
</p><p>The concept that the Hallstatt and La Tène cultures could be seen not just as chronological periods but as "Culture Groups", entities composed of people of the same ethnicity and language, had started to grow by the end of the 19th century. At the beginning of the 20th century the belief that these "Culture Groups" could be thought of in racial or ethnic terms was strongly held by <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Childe" class="mw-redirect" title="Gordon Childe">Gordon Childe</a> whose theory was influenced by the writings of <a href="/wiki/Gustaf_Kossinna" title="Gustaf Kossinna">Gustaf Kossinna</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> As the 20th century progressed, the racial ethnic interpretation of La Tène culture became much more strongly rooted, and any findings of La Tène culture and flat inhumation cemeteries were directly associated with the Celts and the Celtic language.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
The Iron Age <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt_culture" title="Hallstatt culture">Hallstatt</a> (c. 800–475 BC) and <a href="/wiki/La_T%C3%A8ne_culture" title="La Tène culture">La Tène</a> (c. 500–50 BC) cultures are typically associated with Proto-Celtic and Celtic culture.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Celts_in_III_century_BC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Celts_in_III_century_BC.jpg/330px-Celts_in_III_century_BC.jpg" decoding="async" width="330" height="248" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Celts_in_III_century_BC.jpg/495px-Celts_in_III_century_BC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Celts_in_III_century_BC.jpg/660px-Celts_in_III_century_BC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Celts_in_III_century_BC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Expansion of the Celtic culture in the third century BC according to Francisco Villar<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In various<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (July 2010)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/List_of_academic_disciplines" class="mw-redirect" title="List of academic disciplines">academic disciplines</a> the Celts were considered a Central European Iron Age phenomenon, through the cultures of Hallstatt and La Tène. However, archaeological finds from the Halstatt and La Tène culture were rare in the Iberian Peninsula, in southwestern France, northern and western Britain, southern Ireland and Galatia<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> and did not provide enough evidence for a cultural scenario comparable to that of Central Europe. It is considered equally difficult to maintain that the origin of the Peninsular Celts can be linked to the preceding Urnfield culture. This has resulted in a more recent approach that introduces a 'proto-Celtic' substratum and a process of Celticisation, having its initial roots in the Bronze Age <a href="/wiki/Beaker_culture" title="Beaker culture">Bell Beaker culture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lorrio_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lorrio-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The La Tène culture developed and flourished during the late Iron Age (from 450 BC to the Roman conquest in the 1st century BC) in eastern France, Switzerland, Austria, southwest Germany, the Czech Republic, Slovakia and Hungary. It developed out of the Hallstatt culture without any definite cultural break, under the impetus of considerable Mediterranean influence from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a>, and later <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_civilisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Etruscan civilisation">Etruscan civilisations</a>. A shift of settlement centres took place in the 4th century.
</p><p>The western La Tène culture corresponds to historical <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Celtic Gaul</a>. Whether this means that the whole of La Tène culture can be attributed to a unified Celtic people is difficult to assess; archaeologists have repeatedly concluded that language, material culture, and <a href="/wiki/Political_party" title="Political party">political affiliation</a> do not necessarily run parallel. Frey notes that in the 5th century, "burial customs in the Celtic world were not uniform; rather, localised groups had their own beliefs, which, in consequence, also gave rise to distinct artistic expressions".<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Thus, while the La Tène culture is certainly associated with the <a href="/wiki/Gauls" title="Gauls">Gauls</a>, the presence of La Tène artefacts may be due to cultural contact and does not imply the permanent presence of Celtic speakers.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_Gallia_Tribes_Towns.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Map_Gallia_Tribes_Towns.png/330px-Map_Gallia_Tribes_Towns.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="335" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Map_Gallia_Tribes_Towns.png/495px-Map_Gallia_Tribes_Towns.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Map_Gallia_Tribes_Towns.png/660px-Map_Gallia_Tribes_Towns.png 2x" data-file-width="787" data-file-height="799" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_Gallia_Tribes_Towns.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Borders of the region known as <i>Celtica</i> at time of the Roman conquest c. 54 BC; they soon renamed it <i><a href="/wiki/Gallia_Lugdunensis" title="Gallia Lugdunensis">Gallia Lugdunensis</a></i>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Divinka_minca.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Divinka_minca.jpg/220px-Divinka_minca.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Divinka_minca.jpg/330px-Divinka_minca.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Divinka_minca.jpg/440px-Divinka_minca.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5150" data-file-height="3338" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Divinka_minca.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Celtic cointype "Divinka" from <a href="/wiki/Divinka" title="Divinka">Divinka</a> in <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_evidence">Historical evidence</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Polybius" title="Polybius">Polybius</a> published a <a href="/wiki/History_of_Rome" title="History of Rome">history of Rome</a> about 150 BC in which he describes the Gauls of Italy and their conflict with Rome. <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(geographer)" title="Pausanias (geographer)">Pausanias</a> in the 2nd century AD says that the Gauls "originally called Celts", "live on the remotest region of Europe on the coast of an enormous tidal sea". <a href="/wiki/Posidonius" title="Posidonius">Posidonius</a> described the southern Gauls about 100 BC. Though his original work is lost it was used by later writers such as <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>. The latter, writing in the early 1st century AD, deals with Britain and Gaul as well as Hispania, Italy and Galatia. <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Caesar</a> wrote extensively about his <a href="/wiki/Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico" title="Commentarii de Bello Gallico">Gallic Wars</a> in 58–51 BC. <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a> wrote about the Celts of Gaul and Britain in his 1st-century history.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution">Distribution</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Continental_Celts">Continental Celts</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gaul">Gaul</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gauls" title="Gauls">Gauls</a></div>
<p>The Romans knew the Celts then living in what became present-day France as Gauls. The territory of these peoples probably included the <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">Low Countries</a>, the Alps and present-day northern Italy. <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Gallic_Wars" title="Gallic Wars">Gallic Wars</a></i> described the 1st-century BC descendants of those Gauls.
</p><p>Eastern Gaul became the centre of the western La Tène culture. In later Iron Age Gaul, the social organisation resembled that of the Romans, with large towns. From the 3rd century BC the Gauls adopted coinage. Texts with Greek characters from southern Gaul have survived from the 2nd century BC.
</p><p>Greek traders founded <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Massalia</a> about 600 BC, with some objects (mostly drinking ceramics) being traded up the <a href="/wiki/Rh%C3%B4ne_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhône River">Rhone valley</a>. But trade became disrupted soon after 500 BC and re-oriented over the Alps to the Po valley in the Italian peninsula. The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Romans</a> arrived in the Rhone valley in the 2nd century BC and encountered a mostly Celtic-speaking Gaul. Rome wanted land communications with its Iberian provinces and fought a major battle with the <a href="/wiki/Saluvii" class="mw-redirect" title="Saluvii">Saluvii</a> at <a href="/wiki/Entremont_(oppidum)" title="Entremont (oppidum)">Entremont</a> in 124–123 BC. Gradually Roman control extended, and the <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">Roman Province</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gallia_Narbonensis" title="Gallia Narbonensis">Gallia Transalpina</a> developed along the Mediterranean coast.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The Romans knew the remainder of Gaul as Gallia Comata – "Hairy Gaul".
</p><p>In 58 BC the <a href="/wiki/Helvetii" title="Helvetii">Helvetii</a> planned to migrate westward but Julius Caesar forced them back. He then became involved in fighting the various tribes in Gaul, and by 55 BC had overrun most of Gaul. In 52 BC <a href="/wiki/Vercingetorix" title="Vercingetorix">Vercingetorix</a> led a revolt against the Roman occupation but was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Alesia" title="Battle of Alesia">Siege of Alesia</a> and surrendered.
</p><p>Following the Gallic Wars of 58–51 BC, Caesar's <i><a href="/wiki/Gallia_Celtica" title="Gallia Celtica">Celtica</a></i> formed the main part of Roman Gaul, becoming the province of <a href="/wiki/Gallia_Lugdunensis" title="Gallia Lugdunensis">Gallia Lugdunensis</a>. This territory of the Celtic tribes was bounded on the south by the Garonne and on the north by the Seine and the Marne.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The Romans attached large swathes of this region to neighboring provinces <a href="/wiki/Belgica" class="mw-redirect" title="Belgica">Belgica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aquitania" class="mw-redirect" title="Aquitania">Aquitania</a>, particularly under <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a>.
</p><p>Place- and personal-name analysis and inscriptions suggest that the Gaulish Celtic language was spoken over most of what is now France.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Iberia">Iberia</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Iberia_300BC-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Iberia_300BC-en.svg/330px-Iberia_300BC-en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="272" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Iberia_300BC-en.svg/495px-Iberia_300BC-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Iberia_300BC-en.svg/660px-Iberia_300BC-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="924" data-file-height="762" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Iberia_300BC-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Main language areas in <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberia</a>, showing Celtic languages in beige, c. 300 BC</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Celtiberians" title="Celtiberians">Celtiberians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gallaeci" title="Gallaeci">Gallaeci</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Castro_culture" title="Castro culture">Castro culture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pre-Roman_peoples_of_the_Iberian_Peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Pre-Roman peoples of the Iberian Peninsula">Pre-Roman peoples of the Iberian Peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Iberia" title="Prehistoric Iberia">Prehistoric Iberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lusitania" title="Lusitania">Lusitania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gallaecia" title="Gallaecia">Gallaecia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celtici" title="Celtici">Celtici</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vettones" title="Vettones">Vettones</a></div>
<p>Until the end of the 19th century, traditional scholarship dealing with the Celts did acknowledge their presence in the Iberian Peninsula<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> as a <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_culture" title="Archaeological culture">material culture</a> relatable to the <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt" title="Hallstatt">Hallstatt</a> and <a href="/wiki/La_T%C3%A8ne" class="mw-redirect" title="La Tène">La Tène</a> cultures. However, since according to the definition of the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> in the 19th century Celtic populations were supposedly rare in Iberia and did not provide a cultural scenario that could easily be linked to that of Central Europe, the presence of Celtic culture in that region was generally not fully recognised. Modern scholarship, however, has clearly proven that Celtic presence and influences were most substantial in what is today Spain and <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> (with perhaps the highest settlement saturation in Western Europe), particularly in the central, western and northern regions.<sup id="cite_ref-Quintela_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quintela-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to <a href="/wiki/Gauls" title="Gauls">Gauls</a> infiltrating from the north of the <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees</a>, the Roman and Greek sources mention Celtic populations in three parts of the Iberian Peninsula: the eastern part of the <i>Meseta</i> (inhabited by the <a href="/wiki/Celtiberians" title="Celtiberians">Celtiberians</a>), the southwest (<a href="/wiki/Celtici" title="Celtici">Celtici</a>, in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Alentejo" title="Alentejo">Alentejo</a>) and the northwest (<a href="/wiki/Gallaecia" title="Gallaecia">Gallaecia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asturias" title="Asturias">Asturias</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> A modern scholarly review<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> found several archaeological groups of Celts in Spain:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Celtiberians" title="Celtiberians">Celtiberian</a> group in the Upper-Douro Upper-Tagus Upper-Jalón area.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Archaeological data suggest a continuity at least from the 6th century BC. In this early period, the Celtiberians inhabited in hill-forts (<i>Castros</i>). Around the end of the 3rd century BC, Celtiberians adopted more urban ways of life. From the 2nd century BC, they minted coins and wrote inscriptions using the <a href="/wiki/Celtiberian_script" title="Celtiberian script">Celtiberian script</a>. These inscriptions make the <a href="/wiki/Celtiberian_Language" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtiberian Language">Celtiberian Language</a> the only Hispano-Celtic language classified as Celtic with unanimous agreement.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In the late period, before the Roman Conquest, both archaeological evidence and Roman sources suggest that the <a href="/wiki/Celtiberians" title="Celtiberians">Celtiberians</a> were expanding into different areas in the Peninsula (e.g. Celtic Baeturia).</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Vettones" title="Vettones">Vetton</a> group in the western Meseta, between the Tormes, Douro and Tagus Rivers. They were characterised by the production of <i>Verracos</i>, sculptures of bulls and pigs carved in granite.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Vaccei" class="mw-redirect" title="Vaccei">Vaccean</a> group in the central Douro valley. They were mentioned by Roman sources already in the 220 BC. Some of their funerary rituals suggest strong influences from their <a href="/wiki/Celtiberians" title="Celtiberians">Celtiberian</a> neighbours.</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Torque_de_Santa_Tegra_1.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Torque_de_Santa_Tegra_1.JPG/200px-Torque_de_Santa_Tegra_1.JPG" decoding="async" width="200" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Torque_de_Santa_Tegra_1.JPG/300px-Torque_de_Santa_Tegra_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Torque_de_Santa_Tegra_1.JPG/400px-Torque_de_Santa_Tegra_1.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1168" data-file-height="1176" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Torque_de_Santa_Tegra_1.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Triskelion and spirals on a Galician <a href="/wiki/Torc" title="Torc">torc</a> terminal, Museum of Castro de Santa Tegra, <a href="/wiki/A_Guarda" title="A Guarda">A Guarda</a></div></div></div>
<ul><li>The <i>Castro Culture</i> in northwestern Iberia, modern day <a href="/wiki/Galicia_(Spain)" title="Galicia (Spain)">Galicia</a> and Northern Portugal.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Its high degree of continuity, from the Late Bronze Age, makes it difficult to support that the introduction of Celtic elements was due to the same process of Celticization of the western Iberia, from the nucleus area of Celtiberia. Two typical elements are the sauna baths with monumental entrances, and the "Gallaecian Warriors", stone sculptures built in the 1st century AD. A large group of Latin inscriptions contain linguistic features that are clearly Celtic, while others are similar to those found in the non-Celtic <a href="/wiki/Lusitanian_language" title="Lusitanian language">Lusitanian language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Astures" title="Astures">Astures</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cantabri" title="Cantabri">Cantabri</a>. This area was romanised late, as it was not conquered by Rome until the <a href="/wiki/Cantabrian_Wars" title="Cantabrian Wars">Cantabrian Wars</a> of 29–19 BC.</li>
<li>Celts in the southwest, in the area <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> called Celtica <sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The origins of the Celtiberians might provide a key to understanding the Celticisation process in the rest of the Peninsula. The process of Celticisation of the southwestern area of the peninsula by the Keltoi and of the northwestern area is, however, not a simple Celtiberian question. Recent investigations about the <a href="/wiki/Gallaeci" title="Gallaeci">Callaici</a><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bracari" title="Bracari">Bracari</a><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> in northwestern <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> are providing new approaches to understanding Celtic culture (language, art and religion) in western Iberia.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>John T. Koch of <a href="/wiki/Aberystwyth_University" title="Aberystwyth University">Aberystwyth University</a> suggested that <a href="/wiki/Tartessian_language" title="Tartessian language">Tartessian</a> inscriptions of the 8th century BC might be classified as Celtic. This would mean that Tartessian is the earliest attested trace of Celtic by a margin of more than a century.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alps_and_Italy">Alps and Italy</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Golasecca_culture" title="Golasecca culture">Golasecca culture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lepontii" title="Lepontii">Lepontii</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cisalpine_Gaul" title="Cisalpine Gaul">Cisalpine Gaul</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:R%C3%B6mische_Provinzen_im_Alpenraum_ca_14_n_Chr.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/R%C3%B6mische_Provinzen_im_Alpenraum_ca_14_n_Chr.png/330px-R%C3%B6mische_Provinzen_im_Alpenraum_ca_14_n_Chr.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/R%C3%B6mische_Provinzen_im_Alpenraum_ca_14_n_Chr.png/495px-R%C3%B6mische_Provinzen_im_Alpenraum_ca_14_n_Chr.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/R%C3%B6mische_Provinzen_im_Alpenraum_ca_14_n_Chr.png/660px-R%C3%B6mische_Provinzen_im_Alpenraum_ca_14_n_Chr.png 2x" data-file-width="2497" data-file-height="1688" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:R%C3%B6mische_Provinzen_im_Alpenraum_ca_14_n_Chr.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Alpine region of the Roman Empire in 14 AD</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Alps" title="History of the Alps">History of the Alps</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Canegrate_culture" title="Canegrate culture">Canegrate culture</a> represented the first migratory wave of the proto-Celtic<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> population from the northwest part of the Alps that, through the <a href="/wiki/Alpine_passes" class="mw-redirect" title="Alpine passes">Alpine passes</a>, had already penetrated and settled in the western <a href="/wiki/Po_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Po River">Po</a> valley between <a href="/wiki/Lake_Maggiore" title="Lake Maggiore">Lake Maggiore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Como" title="Lake Como">Lake Como</a> (<a href="/wiki/Scamozzina_culture" title="Scamozzina culture">Scamozzina culture</a>). It has also been proposed that a more ancient proto-Celtic presence can be traced back to the beginning of the Middle <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>, when North Westwern Italy appears closely linked regarding the production of bronze artefacts, including ornaments, to the western groups of the <a href="/wiki/Tumulus_culture" title="Tumulus culture">Tumulus culture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> La Tène cultural material appeared over a large area of mainland Italy,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> the southernmost example being the Celtic helmet from <a href="/wiki/Canosa_di_Puglia" title="Canosa di Puglia">Canosa di Puglia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Italy is home to <a href="/wiki/Lepontic_language" title="Lepontic language">Lepontic</a>, the oldest attested Celtic language (from the 6th century BC).<sup id="cite_ref-Schumacher_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schumacher-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Anciently spoken in <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> and in Northern-Central <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, from the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a> to <a href="/wiki/Umbria" title="Umbria">Umbria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> According to the <i>Recueil des Inscriptions Gauloises</i>, more than 760 Gaulish inscriptions have been found throughout present-day <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> – with the notable exception of <a href="/wiki/Aquitaine" title="Aquitaine">Aquitaine</a> – and in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> which testifies the importance of Celtic heritage in the peninsula.
</p><p>In 391 BC, Celts "who had their homes beyond the Alps streamed through the passes in great strength and seized the territory that lay between the <a href="/wiki/Apennine_Mountains" title="Apennine Mountains">Apennine mountains</a> and the Alps" according to <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a>. The <a href="/wiki/River_Po" class="mw-redirect" title="River Po">Po Valley</a> and the rest of northern Italy (known to the Romans as <a href="/wiki/Cisalpine_Gaul" title="Cisalpine Gaul">Cisalpine Gaul</a>) was inhabited by Celtic-speakers who founded cities such as <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Later the Roman army was routed at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Allia" title="Battle of the Allia">battle of Allia</a> and Rome was sacked in 390 BC by the <a href="/wiki/Senones" title="Senones">Senones</a>.
</p><p>At the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Telamon" title="Battle of Telamon">battle of Telamon</a> in 225 BC, a large Celtic army was trapped between two Roman forces and crushed.
</p><p>The defeat of the combined <a href="/wiki/Samnium" title="Samnium">Samnite</a>, Celtic and Etruscan alliance by the Romans in the <a href="/wiki/Samnite_Wars" title="Samnite Wars">Third Samnite War</a> sounded the beginning of the end of the Celtic domination in mainland Europe, but it was not until 192 BC that the Roman armies conquered the last remaining independent Celtic kingdoms in Italy.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_east_and_south">Expansion east and south</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roman_period_tribes_in_Illyria_and_Lower_Pannonia.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Roman_period_tribes_in_Illyria_and_Lower_Pannonia.png/330px-Roman_period_tribes_in_Illyria_and_Lower_Pannonia.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Roman_period_tribes_in_Illyria_and_Lower_Pannonia.png/495px-Roman_period_tribes_in_Illyria_and_Lower_Pannonia.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Roman_period_tribes_in_Illyria_and_Lower_Pannonia.png/660px-Roman_period_tribes_in_Illyria_and_Lower_Pannonia.png 2x" data-file-width="1859" data-file-height="1507" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roman_period_tribes_in_Illyria_and_Lower_Pannonia.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Celtic tribes in S. E. Europe, first century BC (in purple)</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gallic_invasion_of_the_Balkans" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallic invasion of the Balkans">Gallic invasion of the Balkans</a></div>
<p>The Celts also expanded down the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a> river and its tributaries. One of the most influential tribes, the <a href="/wiki/Scordisci" title="Scordisci">Scordisci</a>, had established their capital at <a href="/wiki/Singidunum" title="Singidunum">Singidunum</a> in the 3rd century BC, which is present-day <a href="/wiki/Belgrade" title="Belgrade">Belgrade</a>, Serbia. The concentration of hill-forts and cemeteries shows a <a href="/wiki/Population_density" title="Population density">density of population</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Tisza" title="Tisza">Tisza</a> valley of modern-day <a href="/wiki/Vojvodina" title="Vojvodina">Vojvodina</a>, Serbia, Hungary and into <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>. Expansion into <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> was however blocked by the <a href="/wiki/Dacians" title="Dacians">Dacians</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Serdi" title="Serdi">Serdi</a> were a <a href="/wiki/Celt" class="mw-redirect" title="Celt">Celtic</a> tribe<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> inhabiting <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a>. They were located around  and founded <a href="/wiki/Serdika" title="Serdika">Serdika</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">Bulgarian</a>: <span lang="bg">Сердика</span>, <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Ulpia Serdica</i>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">Σαρδῶν πόλις</span>), now <a href="/wiki/Sofia" title="Sofia">Sofia</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> which reflects their ethnonym. They would have established themselves in this area during the Celtic migrations at the end of the 4th century BC, though there is no evidence for their existence before the 1st century BC. <i>Serdi</i> are among traditional tribal names reported into the Roman era.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> They were gradually Thracianized over the centuries but retained their Celtic character in material culture up to a late date.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (July 2015)">when?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> According to other sources they may have been simply of Thracian origin,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> according to others they may have become of mixed Thraco-Celtic origin. Further south, Celts settled in <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>), which they ruled for over a century, and <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, where they settled as the <a href="/wiki/Galatia" title="Galatia">Galatians</a> <i>(see also: <a href="/wiki/Gallic_invasion_of_the_Balkans" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallic invasion of the Balkans">Gallic Invasion of Greece</a>)</i>. Despite their <a href="/wiki/Geographical_isolation" class="mw-redirect" title="Geographical isolation">geographical isolation</a> from the rest of the Celtic world, the Galatians maintained their Celtic language for at least 700 years. <a href="/wiki/St_Jerome" class="mw-redirect" title="St Jerome">St Jerome</a>, who visited Ancyra (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Ankara" title="Ankara">Ankara</a>) in 373 AD, likened their language to that of the <a href="/wiki/Treveri" title="Treveri">Treveri</a> of northern Gaul.
</p><p>For Venceslas Kruta, Galatia in central Turkey was an area of dense Celtic settlement.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Boii" title="Boii">Boii</a> tribe gave their name to <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a> and possibly <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a>, and Celtic artefacts and cemeteries have been discovered further east in what is now Poland and <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>. A Celtic coin (<a href="/wiki/Biatec" title="Biatec">Biatec</a>) from <a href="/wiki/Bratislava" title="Bratislava">Bratislava</a>'s mint was displayed on the old Slovak 5-crown coin.
</p><p>As there is no archaeological evidence for large-scale invasions in some of the other areas, one current school of thought holds that Celtic language and culture spread to those areas by contact rather than invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> However, the Celtic invasions of Italy and the <a href="/wiki/Gallic_invasion_of_the_Balkans" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallic invasion of the Balkans">expedition in Greece and western Anatolia</a>, are well documented in Greek and Latin history.
</p><p>There are records of Celtic mercenaries in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> serving the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemies" class="mw-redirect" title="Ptolemies">Ptolemies</a>. Thousands were employed in 283–246 BC and they were also in service around 186 BC. They attempted to overthrow <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_II_Philadelphus" title="Ptolemy II Philadelphus">Ptolemy II</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Insular_Celts">Insular Celts</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Romanbritain.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Romanbritain.jpg/260px-Romanbritain.jpg" decoding="async" width="260" height="415" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Romanbritain.jpg/390px-Romanbritain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Romanbritain.jpg/520px-Romanbritain.jpg 2x" data-file-width="854" data-file-height="1364" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Romanbritain.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Principal sites in <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a>, with indication of tribal territories</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Insular_Celts" title="Insular Celts">Insular Celts</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Iron Age Britain">Iron Age Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Celtic_immigration_to_the_British_Isles" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic immigration to the British Isles">Celtic immigration to the British Isles</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age_tribes_in_Britain" title="Iron Age tribes in Britain">Iron Age tribes in Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_substrate_hypothesis" title="Goidelic substrate hypothesis">Goidelic substrate hypothesis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/O%27Rahilly%27s_historical_model" title="O&#39;Rahilly&#39;s historical model">O'Rahilly's historical model</a></div>
<p>All Celtic languages extant today belong to the <a href="/wiki/Insular_Celtic_languages" title="Insular Celtic languages">Insular Celtic languages</a>, derived from the Celtic languages spoken in <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Iron Age Britain">Iron Age Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> They were separated into a <a href="/wiki/Goidelic" class="mw-redirect" title="Goidelic">Goidelic</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Brythonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Brythonic languages">Brythonic</a> branch from an early period.
</p><p>Linguists have been arguing for many years whether a Celtic language came to Britain and Ireland and then split or whether there were two separate "invasions". The older view of prehistorians was that the Celtic influence in the British Isles was the result of successive invasions from the European continent by diverse Celtic-speaking peoples over the course of several centuries, accounting for the <a href="/wiki/P-Celtic" class="mw-redirect" title="P-Celtic">P-Celtic</a> vs. <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages#Classification" title="Celtic languages">Q-Celtic</a> isogloss.
